<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Helium</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the chemical element. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Helium_(disambiguation)" title="Helium (disambiguation)">Helium (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number of 2</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 2</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Helium,  <sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">2</span></sub>He</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Helium_discharge_tube.jpg"><img alt="Helium discharge tube.jpg" data-file-height="2008" data-file-width="3008" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Helium_discharge_tube.jpg/220px-Helium_discharge_tube.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Helium_discharge_tube.jpg/330px-Helium_discharge_tube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Helium_discharge_tube.jpg/440px-Helium_discharge_tube.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#c0ffff">General properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Pronunciation</th><td>
<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span></span>/</a></span></span> <wbr/>​<span class="nowrap">(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">HEE</span>-lee-əm</i></a>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>
colorless gas, exhibiting a red-orange glow when placed in an electric field</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">Standard atomic weight</a><span style="font-weight:normal;"> (<i>A</i><sub>r, standard</sub>)</span></th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_helium" title="Template:Infobox helium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_helium" title="Template talk:Infobox helium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_helium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Helium</b> (from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">ἥλιος</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Greek" title="Romanization of Greek">translit.</a> </small><i lang="el-Latn" title="Greek-language transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">Helios</a></i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a> </small>'Sun') is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>He</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 2. It is a colorless, odorless, tasteless, non-toxic, <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monatomic" title="Monatomic">monatomic</a> <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a>, the first in the <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a> group in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling point</a> is the lowest among all the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a>. After <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, helium is the second lightest and second most <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">abundant element</a> in the observable <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>, being present at about 24% of the total elemental mass, which is more than 12 times the mass of all the heavier elements combined. Its abundance is similar to this figure in the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. This is due to the very high <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">nuclear binding energy</a> (per <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleon</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">helium-4</a> with respect to the next three elements after helium. This helium-4 binding energy also accounts for why it is a product of both <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>. Most helium in the universe is helium-4, the vast majority of which was formed during the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>. Large amounts of new helium are being created by nuclear fusion of hydrogen in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stars" title="Stars">stars</a>.
</p><p>Helium is named for the Greek <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titan</a> of the Sun, <a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">Helios</a>. It was first detected as an unknown yellow <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectral line</a> signature in sunlight during a <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_August_18,_1868" title="Solar eclipse of August 18, 1868">solar eclipse in 1868</a> by <a href="/wiki/Georges_Rayet" title="Georges Rayet">Georges Rayet</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Captain C. T. Haig,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/N._R._Pogson" title="N. R. Pogson">Norman R. Pogson</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and Lieutenant John Herschel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and was subsequently confirmed by French astronomer <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Janssen" title="Pierre Janssen">Jules Janssen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Janssen is often jointly credited with detecting the element along with <a href="/wiki/Norman_Lockyer" title="Norman Lockyer">Norman Lockyer</a>. Janssen recorded the helium spectral line during the solar eclipse of 1868 while Lockyer observed it from Britain. Lockyer was the first to propose that the line was due to a new element, which he named. The formal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discovery_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Discovery of the chemical elements">discovery of the element</a> was made in 1895 by two <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Swedish</a> chemists, <a href="/wiki/Per_Teodor_Cleve" title="Per Teodor Cleve">Per Teodor Cleve</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nils_Abraham_Langlet" title="Nils Abraham Langlet">Nils Abraham Langlet</a>, who found helium emanating from the <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> <a href="/wiki/Cleveite" title="Cleveite">cleveite</a>. In 1903, large reserves of helium were found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_field" title="Natural gas field">natural gas fields</a> in parts of the United States, which is by far the largest supplier of the gas today.
</p><p>Liquid helium is used in <a href="/wiki/Helium_cryogenics" title="Helium cryogenics">cryogenics</a> (its largest single use, absorbing about a quarter of production), particularly in the cooling of <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_magnet" title="Superconducting magnet">superconducting magnets</a>, with the main commercial application being in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI" title="MRI">MRI</a> scanners. Helium's other industrial uses—as a pressurizing and purge gas, as a protective atmosphere for <a href="/wiki/Arc_welding" title="Arc welding">arc welding</a> and in processes such as growing crystals to make <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicon_wafer" title="Silicon wafer">silicon wafers</a>—account for half of the gas produced. A well-known but minor use is as a <a href="/wiki/Lifting_gas" title="Lifting gas">lifting gas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Balloon" title="Balloon">balloons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">airships</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> As with any gas whose density differs from that of air, inhaling a small volume of helium temporarily changes the timbre and quality of the <a href="/wiki/Human_voice" title="Human voice">human voice</a>. In scientific research, the behavior of the two fluid phases of helium-4 (helium I and helium II) is important to researchers studying <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> (in particular the property of <a href="/wiki/Superfluidity" title="Superfluidity">superfluidity</a>) and to those looking at the phenomena, such as <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconductivity</a>, produced in <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> near <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a>.
</p><p>On Earth it is relatively rare—5.2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> by volume in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>. Most terrestrial helium present today is created by the natural <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> of heavy radioactive elements (<a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, although there are other examples), as the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a> emitted by such decays consist of helium-4 <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclei</a>. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiogenic" title="Radiogenic">radiogenic</a> helium is trapped with <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> in concentrations as great as 7% by volume, from which it is extracted commercially by a low-temperature separation process called <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a>. Previously, terrestrial helium—a non-renewable resource, because once released into the atmosphere it readily <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_escape" title="Atmospheric escape">escapes into space</a>—was thought to be in increasingly short supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> However, recent studies suggest that helium produced deep in the earth by radioactive decay can collect in natural gas reserves in larger than expected quantities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> in some cases having been released by volcanic activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sample_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sample-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_discoveries">Scientific discoveries</span></h3>
<p>The first evidence of helium was observed on August 18, 1868, as a bright yellow line with a <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of 587.49 nanometers in the <a href="/wiki/Emission_spectrum" title="Emission spectrum">spectrum</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Chromosphere" title="Chromosphere">chromosphere</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. The line was detected by French astronomer <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Janssen" title="Pierre Janssen">Jules Janssen</a> during <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_August_18,_1868" title="Solar eclipse of August 18, 1868">a total solar eclipse</a> in <a href="/wiki/Guntur" title="Guntur">Guntur</a>, India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frnch_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-frnch-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-18">[18]</a></sup> This line was initially assumed to be <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>. On October 20 of the same year, English astronomer <a href="/wiki/Norman_Lockyer" title="Norman Lockyer">Norman Lockyer</a> observed a yellow line in the solar spectrum, which he named the D<sub>3</sub> because it was near the known D<sub>1</sub> and D<sub>2</sub> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fraunhofer_line" title="Fraunhofer line">Fraunhofer line</a> lines of sodium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lockyer_1868_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lockyer_1868-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup> He concluded that it was caused by an element in the Sun unknown on Earth. Lockyer and English chemist <a href="/wiki/Edward_Frankland" title="Edward Frankland">Edward Frankland</a> named the element with the Greek word for the Sun, ἥλιος (<i><a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">helios</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Helium_spectrum.jpg"><img alt="Picture of visible spectrum with superimposed sharp yellow and blue and violet lines." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="151" data-file-width="506" height="66" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Helium_spectrum.jpg/220px-Helium_spectrum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Helium_spectrum.jpg/330px-Helium_spectrum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Helium_spectrum.jpg/440px-Helium_spectrum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Helium_spectrum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spectral lines of helium</div></div></div>
<p>In 1881, Italian physicist <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Palmieri" title="Luigi Palmieri">Luigi Palmieri</a> detected helium on Earth for the first time through its D<sub>3</sub> spectral line, when he analyzed a material that had been <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimated</a> during a recent eruption of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Vesuvius" title="Mount Vesuvius">Mount Vesuvius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palmieri_1881_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Palmieri_1881-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_Ramsay_working.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1226" data-file-width="800" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/William_Ramsay_working.jpg/170px-William_Ramsay_working.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/William_Ramsay_working.jpg/255px-William_Ramsay_working.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/William_Ramsay_working.jpg/340px-William_Ramsay_working.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William_Ramsay_working.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Ramsay" title="William Ramsay">William Ramsay</a>, the discoverer of terrestrial helium</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clevite_sample_(35321726345).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5656" data-file-width="3585" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Clevite_sample_%2835321726345%29.jpg/170px-Clevite_sample_%2835321726345%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Clevite_sample_%2835321726345%29.jpg/255px-Clevite_sample_%2835321726345%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Clevite_sample_%2835321726345%29.jpg/340px-Clevite_sample_%2835321726345%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clevite_sample_(35321726345).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cleveite sample from which Ramsay first purified helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirk_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirk-24">[24]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>On March 26, 1895, Scottish chemist <a href="/wiki/William_Ramsay" title="William Ramsay">Sir William Ramsay</a> isolated helium on Earth by treating the mineral <a href="/wiki/Cleveite" title="Cleveite">cleveite</a> (a variety of <a href="/wiki/Uraninite" title="Uraninite">uraninite</a> with at least 10% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rare_earth_elements" title="Rare earth elements">rare earth elements</a>) with mineral <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>. Ramsay was looking for <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> but, after separating <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> from the gas liberated by <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, he noticed a bright yellow line that matched the D<sub>3</sub> line observed in the spectrum of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> These samples were identified as helium by Lockyer and British physicist <a href="/wiki/William_Crookes" title="William Crookes">William Crookes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> It was independently isolated from cleveite in the same year by chemists <a href="/wiki/Per_Teodor_Cleve" title="Per Teodor Cleve">Per Teodor Cleve</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Langlet" title="Abraham Langlet">Abraham Langlet</a> in <a href="/wiki/Uppsala" title="Uppsala">Uppsala</a>, Sweden, who collected enough of the gas to accurately determine its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Helium was also isolated by the American geochemist <a href="/wiki/William_Francis_Hillebrand" title="William Francis Hillebrand">William Francis Hillebrand</a> prior to Ramsay's discovery when he noticed unusual spectral lines while testing a sample of the mineral uraninite. Hillebrand, however, attributed the lines to nitrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> His letter of congratulations to Ramsay offers an interesting case of discovery and near-discovery in science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1907, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Royds" title="Thomas Royds">Thomas Royds</a> demonstrated that <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a> are helium <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclei</a> by allowing the particles to penetrate the thin glass wall of an evacuated tube, then creating a discharge in the tube to study the spectrum of the new gas inside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In 1908, helium was first liquefied by Dutch physicist <a href="/wiki/Heike_Kamerlingh_Onnes" title="Heike Kamerlingh Onnes">Heike Kamerlingh Onnes</a> by cooling the gas to less than one <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> He tried to solidify it by further reducing the temperature but failed because helium does not solidify at atmospheric pressure. Onnes' student <a href="/wiki/Willem_Hendrik_Keesom" title="Willem Hendrik Keesom">Willem Hendrik Keesom</a> was eventually able to solidify 1 cm<sup>3</sup> of helium in 1926 by applying additional external pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1913, <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a> published his "trilogy"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyer_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyer-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> on atomic structure that included a reconsideration of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pickering%E2%80%93Fowler_series" title="Pickering–Fowler series">Pickering–Fowler series</a> as central evidence in support of his <a href="/wiki/Bohr_model" title="Bohr model">model of the atom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robotti_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robotti-42">[42]</a></sup> This series is named for <a href="/wiki/Edward_Charles_Pickering" title="Edward Charles Pickering">Edward Charles Pickering</a>, who in 1896 published observations of previously unknown lines in the spectrum of the star <a href="/wiki/Zeta_Puppis" title="Zeta Puppis">ζ Puppis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> (these are now known to occur with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wolf-Rayet_star" title="Wolf-Rayet star">Wolf–Rayet</a> and other hot stars).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Pickering attributed the observation (lines at 4551, 5411, and 10123 <a href="/wiki/%C3%85ngstr%C3%B6m" title="Ångström">Å</a>) to a new form of hydrogen with half-integer transition levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> In 1912, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Fowler" title="Alfred Fowler">Alfred Fowler</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> managed to produce similar lines from a hydrogen-helium mixture, and supported Pickering's conclusion as to their origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Bohr's model does not allow for half-integer transitions (nor does quantum mechanics) and Bohr concluded that Pickering and Fowler were wrong, and instead assigned these spectral lines to ionised helium, He<sup>+</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Fowler was initially skeptical<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> but was ultimately convinced<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> that Bohr was correct,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyer_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyer-39">[39]</a></sup> and by 1915 "spectroscopists had transferred [the Pickering–Fowler series] definitively [from hydrogen] to helium."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robotti_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robotti-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Bohr's theoretical work on the Pickering series had demonstrated the need for "a re-examination of problems that seemed already to have been solved within classical theories" and provided important confirmation for his atomic theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robotti_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Robotti-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1938, Russian physicist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyotr_Leonidovich_Kapitsa" title="Pyotr Leonidovich Kapitsa">Pyotr Leonidovich Kapitsa</a> discovered that <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">helium-4</a> has almost no <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> at temperatures near <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a>, a phenomenon now called <a href="/wiki/Superfluidity" title="Superfluidity">superfluidity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> This phenomenon is related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_condensation" title="Bose–Einstein condensation">Bose–Einstein condensation</a>. In 1972, the same phenomenon was observed in <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">helium-3</a>, but at temperatures much closer to absolute zero, by American physicists <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Douglas_D._Osheroff" title="Douglas D. Osheroff">Douglas D. Osheroff</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_M._Lee" title="David M. Lee">David M. Lee</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Coleman_Richardson" title="Robert Coleman Richardson">Robert C. Richardson</a>. The phenomenon in helium-3 is thought to be related to pairing of helium-3 <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a> to make <a href="/wiki/Boson" title="Boson">bosons</a>, in analogy to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cooper_pairs" title="Cooper pairs">Cooper pairs</a> of electrons producing <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconductivity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraction_and_use">Extraction and use</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kansas_Helium_Marker.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2938" data-file-width="3917" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Kansas_Helium_Marker.jpg/220px-Kansas_Helium_Marker.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Kansas_Helium_Marker.jpg/330px-Kansas_Helium_Marker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Kansas_Helium_Marker.jpg/440px-Kansas_Helium_Marker.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kansas_Helium_Marker.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Historical marker, denoting a massive helium find near <a href="/wiki/Dexter,_Kansas" title="Dexter, Kansas">Dexter, Kansas</a></div></div></div>
<p>After an oil drilling operation in 1903 in <a href="/wiki/Dexter,_Kansas" title="Dexter, Kansas">Dexter, Kansas</a>, produced a gas geyser that would not burn, Kansas state geologist <a href="/wiki/Erasmus_Haworth" title="Erasmus Haworth">Erasmus Haworth</a> collected samples of the escaping gas and took them back to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Kansas" title="University of Kansas">University of Kansas</a> at Lawrence where, with the help of chemists <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Cady" title="Hamilton Cady">Hamilton Cady</a> and David McFarland, he discovered that the gas consisted of, by volume, 72% nitrogen, 15% <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combustible" title="Combustible">combustible</a> percentage only with sufficient oxygen), 1% <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, and 12% an unidentifiable gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> With further analysis, Cady and McFarland discovered that 1.84% of the gas sample was helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> This showed that despite its overall rarity on Earth, helium was concentrated in large quantities under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Great_Plains" title="American Great Plains">American Great Plains</a>, available for extraction as a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>This enabled the United States to become the world's leading supplier of helium. Following a suggestion by Sir <a href="/wiki/Richard_Threlfall" title="Richard Threlfall">Richard Threlfall</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> sponsored three small experimental helium plants during World War I. The goal was to supply <a href="/wiki/Barrage_balloon" title="Barrage balloon">barrage balloons</a> with the non-flammable, lighter-than-air gas. A total of 5,700 m<sup>3</sup> (200,000 cu ft) of 92% helium was produced in the program even though less than a cubic meter of the gas had previously been obtained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup> Some of this gas was used in the world's first helium-filled airship, the U.S. Navy's C-7, which flew its maiden voyage from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hampton_Roads,_Virginia" title="Hampton Roads, Virginia">Hampton Roads, Virginia</a>, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolling_Field" title="Bolling Field">Bolling Field</a> in Washington, D.C., on December 1, 1921,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> nearly two years before the Navy's first <i>rigid</i> helium-filled airship, the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Aircraft_Factory" title="Naval Aircraft Factory">Naval Aircraft Factory</a>-built <a href="/wiki/USS_Shenandoah_(ZR-1)" title="USS Shenandoah (ZR-1)"><i>USS Shenandoah</i></a>, flew in September 1923.
</p><p>Although the extraction process, using low-temperature <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_liquefaction" title="Gas liquefaction">gas liquefaction</a>, was not developed in time to be significant during World War I, production continued. Helium was primarily used as a <a href="/wiki/Lifting_gas" title="Lifting gas">lifting gas</a> in lighter-than-air craft. During World War II, the demand increased for helium for lifting gas and for shielded arc <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Helium_mass_spectrometer" title="Helium mass spectrometer">helium mass spectrometer</a> was also vital in the atomic bomb <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_the_United_States" title="Government of the United States">government of the United States</a> set up the <a href="/wiki/National_Helium_Reserve" title="National Helium Reserve">National Helium Reserve</a> in 1925 at <a href="/wiki/Amarillo,_Texas" title="Amarillo, Texas">Amarillo, Texas</a>, with the goal of supplying military <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">airships</a> in time of war and commercial airships in peacetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup> Because of the <a href="/wiki/Helium_Act_of_1925" title="Helium Act of 1925">Helium Act of 1925</a>, which banned the export of scarce helium on which the US then had a production monopoly, together with the prohibitive cost of the gas, the <a href="/wiki/LZ_129_Hindenburg" title="LZ 129 Hindenburg">Hindenburg</a>, like all German <a href="/wiki/Zeppelin" title="Zeppelin">Zeppelins</a>, was forced to use hydrogen as the lift gas. The helium market after World War II was depressed but the reserve was expanded in the 1950s to ensure a supply of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_helium" title="Liquid helium">liquid helium</a> as a coolant to create oxygen/hydrogen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_fuel" title="Rocket fuel">rocket fuel</a> (among other uses) during the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. Helium use in the United States in 1965 was more than eight times the peak wartime consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the "Helium Acts Amendments of 1960" (Public Law 86–777), the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bureau_of_Mines" title="United States Bureau of Mines">U.S. Bureau of Mines</a> arranged for five private plants to recover helium from natural gas. For this <i>helium conservation</i> program, the Bureau built a 425-mile (684 km) pipeline from <a href="/wiki/Bushton,_Kansas" title="Bushton, Kansas">Bushton, Kansas</a>, to connect those plants with the government's partially depleted Cliffside gas field near Amarillo, Texas. This helium-nitrogen mixture was injected and stored in the Cliffside gas field until needed, at which time it was further purified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1995, a billion cubic meters of the gas had been collected and the reserve was US$1.4 billion in debt, prompting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_United_States" title="Congress of the United States">Congress of the United States</a> in 1996 to phase out the reserve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stwertka_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-stwertka-63">[63]</a></sup> The resulting <a href="/wiki/Helium_Privatization_Act_of_1996" title="Helium Privatization Act of 1996">Helium Privatization Act of 1996</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> (Public Law 104–273) directed the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Interior" title="United States Department of the Interior">United States Department of the Interior</a> to empty the reserve, with sales starting by 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Helium produced between 1930 and 1945 was about 98.3% pure (2% nitrogen), which was adequate for airships. In 1945, a small amount of 99.9% helium was produced for welding use. By 1949, commercial quantities of Grade A 99.95% helium were available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>For many years, the United States produced more than 90% of commercially usable helium in the world, while extraction plants in Canada, Poland, Russia, and other nations produced the remainder. In the mid-1990s, a new plant in <a href="/wiki/Arzew" title="Arzew">Arzew</a>, Algeria, producing 17 million cubic meters (600 million cubic feet) began operation, with enough production to cover all of Europe's demand. Meanwhile, by 2000, the consumption of helium within the U.S. had risen to more than 15 million kg per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In 2004–2006, additional plants in <a href="/wiki/Ras_Laffan_Industrial_City" title="Ras Laffan Industrial City">Ras Laffan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Skikda" title="Skikda">Skikda</a>, Algeria were built. Algeria quickly became the second leading producer of helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwsupply_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-wwsupply-68">[68]</a></sup> Through this time, both helium consumption and the costs of producing helium increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan2007_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan2007-69">[69]</a></sup> From 2002 to 2007 helium prices doubled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basu2007_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Basu2007-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2012, the <a href="/wiki/National_Helium_Reserve" title="National Helium Reserve">United States National Helium Reserve</a> accounted for 30 percent of the world's helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newcomb_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Newcomb-71">[71]</a></sup> The reserve was expected to run out of helium in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newcomb_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Newcomb-71">[71]</a></sup> Despite that, a proposed bill in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a> would allow the reserve to continue to sell the gas. Other large reserves were in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hugoton_Natural_Gas_Area" title="Hugoton Natural Gas Area">Hugoton</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>, United States, and nearby gas fields of Kansas and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panhandles" title="Panhandles">panhandles</a> of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>. New helium plants were scheduled to open in 2012 in <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>, Russia, and the US state of <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>, but they were not expected to ease the shortage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newcomb_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-Newcomb-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, Qatar started up the world's largest helium unit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> although the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2017_Qatar_diplomatic_crisis" title="2017 Qatar diplomatic crisis">2017 Qatar diplomatic crisis</a> severely affected helium production there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> 2014 was widely acknowledged to be a year of over-supply in the helium business, following years of renowned shortages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Nasdaq reported (2015) that for <a href="/wiki/Air_Products_%26_Chemicals" title="Air Products &amp; Chemicals">Air Products</a>, an international corporation that sells gases for industrial use, helium volumes remain under economic pressure due to feedstock supply constraints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_helium_atom">The helium atom</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Helium_atom" title="Helium atom">Helium atom</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Helium_atom_QM.svg"><img alt="Picture of a diffuse gray sphere with grayscale density decreasing from the center. Length scale about 1 Angstrom. An inset outlines the structure of the core, with two red and two blue atoms at the length scale of 1 femtometer." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="667" data-file-width="665" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Helium_atom_QM.svg/220px-Helium_atom_QM.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Helium_atom_QM.svg/330px-Helium_atom_QM.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Helium_atom_QM.svg/440px-Helium_atom_QM.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Helium_atom_QM.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>The helium atom.</b> Depicted are the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> (pink) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_cloud" title="Electron cloud">electron cloud</a> distribution (black). The nucleus (upper right) in helium-4 is in reality spherically symmetric and closely resembles the electron cloud, although for more complicated nuclei this is not always the case.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Helium_in_quantum_mechanics">Helium in quantum mechanics</span></h4>
<p>In the perspective of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, helium is the second simplest <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a> to model, following the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a>. Helium is composed of two electrons in <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">atomic orbitals</a> surrounding a nucleus containing two protons and (usually) two neutrons. As in Newtonian mechanics, no system that consists of more than two particles can be solved with an exact analytical mathematical approach (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3-body_problem" title="3-body problem">3-body problem</a>) and helium is no exception. Thus, numerical mathematical methods are required, even to solve the system of one nucleus and two electrons. Such <a href="/wiki/Computational_chemistry" title="Computational chemistry">computational chemistry</a> methods have been used to create a quantum mechanical picture of helium electron binding which is accurate to within &lt; 2% of the correct value, in a few computational steps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Such models show that each electron in helium partly screens the nucleus from the other, so that the effective nuclear charge <i>Z</i> which each electron sees, is about 1.69 units, not the 2 charges of a classic "bare" helium nucleus.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_related_stability_of_the_helium-4_nucleus_and_electron_shell">The related stability of the helium-4 nucleus and electron shell</span></h4>
<p>The nucleus of the helium-4 atom is identical with an <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a>. High-energy electron-scattering experiments show its charge to decrease exponentially from a maximum at a central point, exactly as does the charge density of helium's own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_cloud" title="Electron cloud">electron cloud</a>. This symmetry reflects similar underlying physics: the pair of neutrons and the pair of protons in helium's nucleus obey the same quantum mechanical rules as do helium's pair of electrons (although the nuclear particles are subject to a different nuclear binding potential), so that all these <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a> fully occupy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1s1s" title="1s1s">1s</a> orbitals in pairs, none of them possessing orbital angular momentum, and each cancelling the other's intrinsic spin. Adding another of any of these particles would require angular momentum and would release substantially less energy (in fact, no nucleus with five nucleons is stable). This arrangement is thus energetically extremely stable for all these particles, and this stability accounts for many crucial facts regarding helium in nature.
</p><p>For example, the stability and low energy of the electron cloud state in helium accounts for the element's chemical inertness, and also the lack of interaction of helium atoms with each other, producing the lowest melting and boiling points of all the elements.
</p><p>In a similar way, the particular energetic stability of the helium-4 nucleus, produced by similar effects, accounts for the ease of helium-4 production in atomic reactions that involve either heavy-particle emission or fusion. Some stable helium-3 (2 protons and 1 neutron) is produced in fusion reactions from hydrogen, but it is a very small fraction compared to the highly favorable helium-4.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="441" data-file-width="671" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/220px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/330px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/440px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Binding energy per nucleon of common isotopes. The binding energy per particle of helium-4 is significantly larger than all nearby nuclides.</div></div></div>
<p>The unusual stability of the helium-4 nucleus is also important <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmologically</a>: it explains the fact that in the first few minutes after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, as the "soup" of free protons and neutrons which had initially been created in about 6:1 ratio cooled to the point that nuclear binding was possible, almost all first compound atomic nuclei to form were helium-4 nuclei. So tight was helium-4 binding that helium-4 production consumed nearly all of the free neutrons in a few minutes, before they could beta-decay, and also leaving few to form heavier atoms such as lithium, beryllium, or boron. Helium-4 nuclear binding per nucleon is stronger than in any of these elements (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleogenesis" title="Nucleogenesis">nucleogenesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a>) and thus, once helium had been formed, no energetic drive was available to make elements 3, 4 and 5. It was barely energetically favorable for helium to fuse into the next element with a lower energy per <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleon</a>, carbon. However, due to lack of intermediate elements, this process requires three helium nuclei striking each other nearly simultaneously (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triple_alpha_process" title="Triple alpha process">triple alpha process</a>). There was thus no time for significant carbon to be formed in the few minutes after the Big Bang, before the early expanding universe cooled to the temperature and pressure point where helium fusion to carbon was no longer possible. This left the early universe with a very similar ratio of hydrogen/helium as is observed today (3 parts hydrogen to 1 part helium-4 by mass), with nearly all the neutrons in the universe trapped in helium-4.
</p><p>All heavier elements (including those necessary for rocky planets like the Earth, and for carbon-based or other life) have thus been created since the Big Bang in stars which were hot enough to fuse helium itself. All elements other than hydrogen and helium today account for only 2% of the mass of atomic matter in the universe. Helium-4, by contrast, makes up about 23% of the universe's ordinary matter—nearly all the ordinary matter that is not hydrogen.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gas_and_plasma_phases">Gas and plasma phases</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HeTube.jpg"><img alt="Illuminated light red gas discharge tubes shaped as letters H and e" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2000" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/HeTube.jpg/170px-HeTube.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/HeTube.jpg/255px-HeTube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/HeTube.jpg/340px-HeTube.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HeTube.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Helium discharge tube shaped like the element's atomic symbol</div></div></div>
<p>Helium is the second least reactive noble gas after <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>, and thus the second least reactive of all elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> It is <a href="/wiki/Chemically_inert" title="Chemically inert">chemically inert</a> and monatomic in all standard conditions. Because of helium's relatively low molar (atomic) mass, its <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat" title="Specific heat">specific heat</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">sound speed</a> in the gas phase are all greater than any other gas except <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>. For these reasons and the small size of helium monatomic molecules, helium <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffuses</a> through solids at a rate three times that of air and around 65% that of hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Helium is the least water-<a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">soluble</a> monatomic gas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> and one of the least water-soluble of any gas (CF<sub>4</sub>, SF<sub>6</sub>, and C<sub>4</sub>F<sub>8</sub> have lower mole fraction solubilities: 0.3802, 0.4394, and 0.2372 x<sub>2</sub>/10<sup>−5</sup>, respectively, versus helium's 0.70797 x<sub>2</sub>/10<sup>−5</sup>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> and helium's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Index_of_refraction" title="Index of refraction">index of refraction</a> is closer to unity than that of any other gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Helium has a negative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joule%E2%80%93Thomson_coefficient" title="Joule–Thomson coefficient">Joule–Thomson coefficient</a> at normal ambient temperatures, meaning it heats up when allowed to freely expand. Only below its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joule%E2%80%93Thomson_inversion_temperature" title="Joule–Thomson inversion temperature">Joule–Thomson inversion temperature</a> (of about 32 to 50 K at 1 atmosphere) does it cool upon free expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup> Once precooled below this temperature, helium can be liquefied through expansion cooling.
</p><p>Most extraterrestrial helium is found in a <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> state, with properties quite different from those of atomic helium. In a plasma, helium's electrons are not bound to its nucleus, resulting in very high electrical conductivity, even when the gas is only partially ionized. The charged particles are highly influenced by magnetic and electric fields. For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> together with ionized hydrogen, the particles interact with the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>, giving rise to <a href="/wiki/Birkeland_current" title="Birkeland current">Birkeland currents</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aurora" title="Aurora">aurora</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquid_helium">Liquid helium</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2_Helium.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="215" data-file-width="400" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/2_Helium.png/220px-2_Helium.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/2_Helium.png/330px-2_Helium.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/2_Helium.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2_Helium.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Liquefied helium. This helium is not only liquid, but has been cooled to the point of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superfluid" title="Superfluid">superfluidity</a>. The drop of liquid at the bottom of the glass represents helium spontaneously escaping from the container over the side, to empty out of the container. The energy to drive this process is supplied by the potential energy of the falling helium.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liquid_helium" title="Liquid helium">Liquid helium</a></div>
<p>Unlike any other element, helium will remain liquid down to <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a> at normal pressures. This is a direct effect of quantum mechanics: specifically, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zero_point_energy" title="Zero point energy">zero point energy</a> of the system is too high to allow freezing. Solid helium requires a temperature of 1–1.5 K (about −272 °C or −457 °F) at about 25 bar (2.5 MPa) of pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> It is often hard to distinguish solid from liquid helium since the <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> of the two phases are nearly the same. The solid has a sharp <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a> and has a <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystalline</a> structure, but it is highly <a href="/wiki/Compressibility" title="Compressibility">compressible</a>; applying pressure in a laboratory can decrease its volume by more than 30%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL.gov_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-LANL.gov-83">[83]</a></sup> With a <a href="/wiki/Bulk_modulus" title="Bulk modulus">bulk modulus</a> of about 27 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megapascal" title="Megapascal">MPa</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> it is ~100 times more compressible than water. Solid helium has a density of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999214000000000000♠</span>0.214<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.006 g/cm<sup>3</sup></span> at 1.15 K and 66 atm; the projected density at 0 K and 25 bar (2.5 MPa) is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999187000000000000♠</span>0.187<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.009 g/cm<sup>3</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> At higher temperatures, helium will solidify with sufficient pressure. At room temperature, this requires about 114,000 atm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chemicool_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-chemicool-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Helium_I">Helium I</span></h4>
<p>Below its <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling point</a> of 4.22 kelvins and above the <a href="/wiki/Lambda_point" title="Lambda point">lambda point</a> of 2.1768 kelvins, the <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> helium-4 exists in a normal colorless liquid state, called <i>helium I</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-6"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup> Like other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogenic" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> liquids, helium I boils when it is heated and contracts when its temperature is lowered. Below the lambda point, however, helium does not boil, and it expands as the temperature is lowered further. 
</p><p>Helium I has a gas-like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Index_of_refraction" title="Index of refraction">index of refraction</a> of 1.026 which makes its surface so hard to see that floats of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expanded_polystyrene" title="Expanded polystyrene">Styrofoam</a> are often used to show where the surface is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-7"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup> This colorless liquid has a very low <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> and a density of 0.145–0.125 g/mL (between about 0 and 4 K),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crc6120_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-crc6120-87">[87]</a></sup> which is only one-fourth the value expected from <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">classical physics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-8"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">Quantum mechanics</a> is needed to explain this property and thus both states of liquid helium (helium I and helium II) are called <i>quantum fluids</i>, meaning they display atomic properties on a macroscopic scale. This may be an effect of its boiling point being so close to absolute zero, preventing random molecular motion (<a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a>) from masking the atomic properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-9"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Helium_II">Helium II</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Superfluid_helium-4" title="Superfluid helium-4">Superfluid helium-4</a></div>
<p>Liquid helium below its lambda point (called <i>helium II</i>) exhibits very unusual characteristics. Due to its high <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>, when it boils, it does not bubble but rather evaporates directly from its surface. <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">Helium-3</a> also has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superfluid" title="Superfluid">superfluid</a> phase, but only at much lower temperatures; as a result, less is known about the properties of the isotope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-10"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Helium-II-creep.svg"><img alt="A cross-sectional drawing showing one vessel inside another. There is a liquid in the outer vessel, and it tends to flow into the inner vessel over its walls." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="357" data-file-width="257" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Helium-II-creep.svg/170px-Helium-II-creep.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Helium-II-creep.svg/255px-Helium-II-creep.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Helium-II-creep.svg/340px-Helium-II-creep.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Helium-II-creep.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unlike ordinary liquids, helium II will creep along surfaces in order to reach an equal level; after a short while, the levels in the two containers will equalize. The <a href="/wiki/Rollin_film" title="Rollin film">Rollin film</a> also covers the interior of the larger container; if it were not sealed, the helium II would creep out and escape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-11"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Helium II is a superfluid, a quantum mechanical state (see: <a href="/wiki/Macroscopic_quantum_phenomena" title="Macroscopic quantum phenomena">macroscopic quantum phenomena</a>) of matter with strange properties. For example, when it flows through capillaries as thin as 10<sup>−7</sup> to 10<sup>−8</sup> m it has no measurable <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-18">[18]</a></sup> However, when measurements were done between two moving discs, a viscosity comparable to that of gaseous helium was observed. Current theory explains this using the <i>two-fluid model</i> for helium II. In this model, liquid helium below the lambda point is viewed as containing a proportion of helium atoms in a <a href="/wiki/Ground_state" title="Ground state">ground state</a>, which are superfluid and flow with exactly zero viscosity, and a proportion of helium atoms in an excited state, which behave more like an ordinary fluid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i>fountain effect</i>, a chamber is constructed which is connected to a reservoir of helium II by a <a href="/wiki/Sintering" title="Sintering">sintered</a> disc through which superfluid helium leaks easily but through which non-superfluid helium cannot pass. If the interior of the container is heated, the superfluid helium changes to non-superfluid helium. In order to maintain the equilibrium fraction of superfluid helium, superfluid helium leaks through and increases the pressure, causing liquid to fountain out of the container.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>The thermal conductivity of helium II is greater than that of any other known substance, a million times that of helium I and several hundred times that of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-12"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup> This is because heat conduction occurs by an exceptional quantum mechanism. Most materials that conduct heat well have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valence_band" title="Valence band">valence band</a> of free electrons which serve to transfer the heat. Helium II has no such valence band but nevertheless conducts heat well. The <a href="/wiki/Heat_transfer" title="Heat transfer">flow of heat</a> is governed by equations that are similar to the <a href="/wiki/Wave_equation" title="Wave equation">wave equation</a> used to characterize sound propagation in air. When heat is introduced, it moves at 20 meters per second at 1.8 K through helium II as waves in a phenomenon known as <i><a href="/wiki/Second_sound" title="Second sound">second sound</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-13"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Helium II also exhibits a creeping effect. When a surface extends past the level of helium II, the helium II moves along the surface, against the force of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. Helium II will escape from a vessel that is not sealed by creeping along the sides until it reaches a warmer region where it evaporates. It moves in a 30 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a>-thick film regardless of surface material. This film is called a <a href="/wiki/Rollin_film" title="Rollin film">Rollin film</a> and is named after the man who first characterized this trait, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bernard_V._Rollin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bernard V. Rollin (page does not exist)">Bernard V. Rollin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-14"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> As a result of this creeping behavior and helium II's ability to leak rapidly through tiny openings, it is very difficult to confine liquid helium. Unless the container is carefully constructed, the helium II will creep along the surfaces and through valves until it reaches somewhere warmer, where it will evaporate. Waves propagating across a Rollin film are governed by the same equation as <a href="/wiki/Gravity_wave" title="Gravity wave">gravity waves</a> in shallow water, but rather than gravity, the restoring force is the <a href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_force" title="Van der Waals force">van der Waals force</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> These waves are known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_sound" title="Third sound">third sound</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_helium" title="Isotopes of helium">Isotopes of helium</a></div>
<p>There are nine known <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> of helium, but only <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">helium-3</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">helium-4</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stable_isotope" title="Stable isotope">stable</a>. In the Earth's atmosphere, one atom is <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>He</span> for every million that are <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>He</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-18">[18]</a></sup> Unlike most elements, helium's isotopic abundance varies greatly by origin, due to the different formation processes. The most common isotope, helium-4, is produced on Earth by <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha decay</a> of heavier radioactive elements; the alpha particles that emerge are fully ionized helium-4 nuclei. Helium-4 is an unusually stable nucleus because its <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a> are arranged into <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_shell_model" title="Nuclear shell model">complete shells</a>. It was also formed in enormous quantities during <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bigbang_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-bigbang-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Helium-3 is present on Earth only in trace amounts. Most of it has been present since Earth's formation, though some falls to Earth trapped in <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">cosmic dust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heliumfundamentals_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-heliumfundamentals-95">[95]</a></sup> Trace amounts are also produced by the <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Rocks from the Earth's crust have isotope ratios varying by as much as a factor of ten, and these ratios can be used to investigate the origin of rocks and the composition of the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heliumfundamentals_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-heliumfundamentals-95">[95]</a></sup> <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>He</span> is much more abundant in stars as a product of nuclear fusion. Thus in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>, the proportion of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>He</span> to <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>He</span> is about 100 times higher than on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Extraplanetary material, such as <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">lunar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">regolith</a>, have trace amounts of helium-3 from being bombarded by <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar winds</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>'s surface contains helium-3 at concentrations on the order of 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" title="Parts per billion">ppb</a>, much higher than the approximately 5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_trillion" title="Parts per trillion">ppt</a> found in the Earth's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> A number of people, starting with Gerald Kulcinski in 1986,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> have proposed to explore the moon, mine lunar regolith, and use the helium-3 for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">fusion</a>.
</p><p>Liquid helium-4 can be cooled to about 1 kelvin using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evaporative_cooling" title="Evaporative cooling">evaporative cooling</a> in a <a href="/wiki/1-K_pot" title="1-K pot">1-K pot</a>. Similar cooling of helium-3, which has a lower boiling point, can achieve about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999200000000000000♠</span>0.2 kelvin</span> in a <a href="/wiki/Helium-3_refrigerator" title="Helium-3 refrigerator">helium-3 refrigerator</a>. Equal mixtures of liquid <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>He</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>He</span> below <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999800000000000000♠</span>0.8 K</span> separate into two immiscible phases due to their dissimilarity (they follow different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_statistics" title="Quantum statistics">quantum statistics</a>: helium-4 atoms are <a href="/wiki/Boson" title="Boson">bosons</a> while helium-3 atoms are <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-15"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dilution_refrigerator" title="Dilution refrigerator">Dilution refrigerators</a> use this immiscibility to achieve temperatures of a few millikelvins.
</p><p>It is possible to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exotic_helium_isotopes" title="Exotic helium isotopes">exotic helium isotopes</a>, which rapidly decay into other substances. The shortest-lived heavy helium isotope is helium-5 with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6978760000000000000♠</span>7.6<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−22</sup> s</span>. Helium-6 decays by emitting a <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta particle</a> and has a half-life of 0.8 second. Helium-7 also emits a beta particle as well as a <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a>. Helium-7 and helium-8 are created in certain <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reactions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-16"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup> Helium-6 and helium-8 are known to exhibit a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_halo" title="Nuclear halo">nuclear halo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-17"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Helium_compounds" title="Helium compounds">Helium compounds</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Helium-hydride-cation-3D-SF.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="934" data-file-width="979" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Helium-hydride-cation-3D-SF.png/220px-Helium-hydride-cation-3D-SF.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Helium-hydride-cation-3D-SF.png/330px-Helium-hydride-cation-3D-SF.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Helium-hydride-cation-3D-SF.png/440px-Helium-hydride-cation-3D-SF.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Helium-hydride-cation-3D-SF.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of the <a href="/wiki/Helium_hydride_ion" title="Helium hydride ion">helium hydride ion</a>, HHe<sup>+</sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fluoroheliate-ion-3D-vdW.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="449" data-file-width="779" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Fluoroheliate-ion-3D-vdW.png/220px-Fluoroheliate-ion-3D-vdW.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Fluoroheliate-ion-3D-vdW.png/330px-Fluoroheliate-ion-3D-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Fluoroheliate-ion-3D-vdW.png/440px-Fluoroheliate-ion-3D-vdW.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fluoroheliate-ion-3D-vdW.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of the suspected fluoroheliate anion, OHeF<sup>−</sup></div></div></div>
<p>Helium has a <a href="/wiki/Valence_(chemistry)" title="Valence (chemistry)">valence</a> of zero and is chemically unreactive under all normal conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL.gov_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-LANL.gov-83">[83]</a></sup> It is an electrical insulator unless <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionized</a>. As with the other noble gases, helium has metastable <a href="/wiki/Energy_level" title="Energy level">energy levels</a> that allow it to remain ionized in an electrical discharge with a <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltage</a> below its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionization_potential" title="Ionization potential">ionization potential</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-18"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup> Helium can form unstable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compound_(chemistry)" title="Compound (chemistry)">compounds</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Excimer" title="Excimer">excimers</a>, with tungsten, iodine, fluorine, sulfur, and phosphorus when it is subjected to a <a href="/wiki/Glow_discharge" title="Glow discharge">glow discharge</a>, to electron bombardment, or reduced to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_physics" title="Plasma physics">plasma</a> by other means. The molecular compounds HeNe, HgHe<sub>10</sub>, and WHe<sub>2</sub>, and the molecular ions <span class="chemf nowrap">He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Helium_hydride_ion" title="Helium hydride ion">HeH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">HeD<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> have been created this way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> HeH<sup>+</sup> is also stable in its ground state, but is extremely reactive—it is the strongest <a href="/wiki/Br%C3%B8nsted%E2%80%93Lowry_acid%E2%80%93base_theory" title="Brønsted–Lowry acid–base theory">Brønsted acid</a> known, and therefore can exist only in isolation, as it will protonate any molecule or counteranion it contacts. This technique has also produced the neutral molecule He<sub>2</sub>, which has a large number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectral_band" title="Spectral band">band systems</a>, and HgHe, which is apparently held together only by polarization forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-19"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_compound" title="Van der Waals compound">Van der Waals compounds</a> of helium can also be formed with cryogenic helium gas and atoms of some other substance, such as <a href="/wiki/LiHe" title="LiHe">LiHe</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dihelium" title="Dihelium">He<sub>2</sub></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fr13_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-fr13-102">[102]</a></sup>
