</p><p>Theoretically, other true compounds may be possible, such as helium fluorohydride (HHeF) which would be analogous to <a href="/wiki/Argon_fluorohydride" title="Argon fluorohydride">HArF</a>, discovered in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Calculations show that two new compounds containing a helium-oxygen bond could be stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Two new molecular species, predicted using theory, CsFHeO and N(CH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub>FHeO, are derivatives of a metastable FHeO<sup>−</sup> anion first theorized in 2005 by a group from Taiwan. If confirmed by experiment, the only remaining element with no known stable compounds would be <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Helium atoms have been inserted into the hollow carbon cage molecules (the <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">fullerenes</a>) by heating under high pressure. The <a href="/wiki/Endohedral_fullerene" title="Endohedral fullerene">endohedral fullerene molecules</a> formed are stable at high temperatures. When chemical derivatives of these fullerenes are formed, the helium stays inside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> If <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">helium-3</a> is used, it can be readily observed by helium <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance_spectroscopy" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy">nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Many fullerenes containing helium-3 have been reported. Although the helium atoms are not attached by covalent or ionic bonds, these substances have distinct properties and a definite composition, like all stoichiometric chemical compounds.
</p><p>Under high pressures helium can form compounds with various other elements. Helium-nitrogen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clathrate" title="Clathrate">clathrate</a> (He(N<sub>2</sub>)<sub>11</sub>) crystals have been grown at room temperature at pressures ca. 10 GPa in a <a href="/wiki/Diamond_anvil_cell" title="Diamond anvil cell">diamond anvil cell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Insulator_(electricity)" title="Insulator (electricity)">insulating</a> <a href="/wiki/Electride" title="Electride">electride</a> Na<sub>2</sub>He has been shown to be thermodynamically stable at pressures above 113 GPa. It has a <a href="/wiki/Fluorite" title="Fluorite">fluorite</a> structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DongOganov2017_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-DongOganov2017-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_production">Occurrence and production</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_abundance">Natural abundance</span></h3>
<p>Although it is rare on Earth, helium is the second most abundant element in the known Universe (after <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>), constituting 23% of its <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryonic</a> mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-18">[18]</a></sup> The vast majority of helium was formed by <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a> one to three minutes after the Big Bang. As such, measurements of its abundance contribute to cosmological models. In <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, it is formed by the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> of hydrogen in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton-proton_chain_reaction" title="Proton-proton chain reaction">proton-proton chain reactions</a> and the <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">CNO cycle</a>, part of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bigbang_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-bigbang-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>, the concentration of helium by volume is only 5.2 parts per million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The concentration is low and fairly constant despite the continuous production of new helium because most helium in the Earth's atmosphere <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_escape" title="Atmospheric escape">escapes into space</a> by several processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TalkOriginsCreationism_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-TalkOriginsCreationism-114">[114]</a></sup> In the Earth's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterosphere" title="Heterosphere">heterosphere</a>, a part of the upper atmosphere, helium and other lighter gases are the most abundant elements.
</p><p>Most helium on Earth is a result of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>. Helium is found in large amounts in minerals of <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Cleveite" title="Cleveite">cleveite</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitchblende" title="Pitchblende">pitchblende</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carnotite" title="Carnotite">carnotite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monazite" title="Monazite">monazite</a>, because they emit alpha particles (helium nuclei, He<sup>2+</sup>) to which electrons immediately combine as soon as the particle is stopped by the rock. In this way an estimated 3000 metric tons of helium are generated per year throughout the <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cook_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-cook-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> In the Earth's crust, the concentration of helium is 8 parts per billion. In seawater, the concentration is only 4 parts per trillion. There are also small amounts in mineral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spring_(hydrosphere)" title="Spring (hydrosphere)">springs</a>, volcanic gas, and <a href="/wiki/Meteoric_iron" title="Meteoric iron">meteoric iron</a>. Because helium is trapped in the subsurface under conditions that also trap natural gas, the greatest natural concentrations of helium on the planet are found in natural gas, from which most commercial helium is extracted. The concentration varies in a broad range from a few ppm to more than 7% in a small gas field in <a href="/wiki/San_Juan_County,_New_Mexico" title="San Juan County, New Mexico">San Juan County, New Mexico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2011 the world's helium reserves were estimated at 40 billion cubic meters, with a quarter of that being in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Pars_/_North_Dome_Gas-Condensate_field" title="South Pars / North Dome Gas-Condensate field">South Pars / North Dome Gas-Condensate field</a> owned jointly by <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a> and Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> In 2015 and 2016 more probable reserves were announced to be under the Rocky Mountains in North America<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> and in east Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_extraction_and_distribution">Modern extraction and distribution</span></h3>
<p>For large-scale use, helium is extracted by <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a> from natural gas, which can contain as much as 7% helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Since helium has a lower <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling point</a> than any other element, low temperature and high pressure are used to liquefy nearly all the other gases (mostly <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>). The resulting crude helium gas is purified by successive exposures to lowering temperatures, in which almost all of the remaining nitrogen and other gases are precipitated out of the gaseous mixture. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Activated_charcoal" title="Activated charcoal">Activated charcoal</a> is used as a final purification step, usually resulting in 99.995% pure Grade-A helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-20"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup> The principal impurity in Grade-A helium is <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>. In a final production step, most of the helium that is produced is liquefied via a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogenic" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> process. This is necessary for applications requiring liquid helium and also allows helium suppliers to reduce the cost of long distance transportation, as the largest liquid helium containers have more than five times the capacity of the largest gaseous helium tube trailers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwsupply_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-wwsupply-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008, approximately 169 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_cubic_meter" title="Standard cubic meter">standard cubic meters</a> (SCM) of helium were extracted from natural gas or withdrawn from helium reserves with approximately 78% from the United States, 10% from Algeria, and most of the remainder from Russia, Poland and Qatar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> By 2013, increases in helium production in Qatar (under the company <a href="/wiki/RasGas" title="RasGas">RasGas</a> managed by <a href="/wiki/Air_Liquide" title="Air Liquide">Air Liquide</a>) had increased Qatar's fraction of world helium production to 25%, and made it the second largest exporter after the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloomberg.com_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg.com-126">[126]</a></sup>
An estimated 54 billion cubic feet (1.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> m<sup>3</sup>) deposit of helium was found in Tanzania in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sample_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sample-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, most helium is extracted from natural gas of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hugoton_Natural_Gas_Area" title="Hugoton Natural Gas Area">Hugoton</a> and nearby gas fields in Kansas, Oklahoma, and the Panhandle Field in Texas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwsupply_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-wwsupply-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Much of this gas was once sent by pipeline to the <a href="/wiki/National_Helium_Reserve" title="National Helium Reserve">National Helium Reserve</a>, but since 2005 this reserve is being depleted and sold off, and is expected to be largely depleted by 2021,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloomberg.com_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg.com-126">[126]</a></sup> under the October 2013 <i>Responsible Helium Administration and Stewardship Act</i> (H.R. 527).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Diffusion of crude natural gas through special <a href="/wiki/Semipermeable_membrane" title="Semipermeable membrane">semipermeable membranes</a> and other barriers is another method to recover and purify helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> In 1996, the U.S. had <i>proven</i> helium reserves, in such gas well complexes, of about 147 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_cubic_feet" title="Standard cubic feet">standard cubic feet</a> (4.2 billion SCM).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> At rates of use at that time (72 million SCM per year in the U.S.; see pie chart below) this would have been enough helium for about 58 years of U.S. use, and less than this (perhaps 80% of the time) at world use rates, although factors in saving and processing impact effective reserve numbers.
</p><p>Helium must be extracted from natural gas because it is present in air at only a fraction of that of neon, yet the demand for it is far higher. It is estimated that if all neon production were retooled to save helium, that 0.1% of the world's helium demands would be satisfied. Similarly, only 1% of the world's helium demands could be satisfied by re-tooling all air distillation plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Helium can be synthesized by bombardment of <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> with high-velocity protons, or by bombardment of lithium with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuteron" title="Deuteron">deuterons</a>, but these processes are a completely uneconomical method of production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Helium is commercially available in either liquid or gaseous form. As a liquid, it can be supplied in small insulated containers called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dewar_flask" title="Dewar flask">dewars</a> which hold as much as 1,000 liters of helium, or in large ISO containers which have nominal capacities as large as 42 m<sup>3</sup> (around 11,000 U.S. <a href="/wiki/Gallon" title="Gallon">gallons</a>). In gaseous form, small quantities of helium are supplied in high-pressure cylinders holding as much as 8 m<sup>3</sup> (approx. 282 standard cubic feet), while large quantities of high-pressure gas are supplied in tube trailers which have capacities of as much as 4,860 m<sup>3</sup> (approx. 172,000 standard cubic feet).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_advocates">Conservation advocates</span></h3>
<p>According to helium conservationists like Nobel laureate physicist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Coleman_Richardson" title="Robert Coleman Richardson">Robert Coleman Richardson</a>, writing in 2010, the free market price of helium has contributed to "wasteful" usage (e.g. for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tethered_helium_balloon" title="Tethered helium balloon">helium balloons</a>). Prices in the 2000s had been lowered by the decision of the U.S. Congress to sell off the country's large helium stockpile by 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> According to Richardson, the price needed to be multiplied by 20 to eliminate the excessive wasting of helium. In their book, the <i>Future of helium as a natural resource</i> (Routledge, 2012), Nuttall, Clarke &amp; Glowacki (2012) also proposed to create an International Helium Agency (IHA) to build a sustainable market for this precious commodity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Modern_3T_MRI.JPG"><img alt="A large solid cylinder with a hole in its center and a rail attached to its side." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Modern_3T_MRI.JPG/220px-Modern_3T_MRI.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Modern_3T_MRI.JPG/330px-Modern_3T_MRI.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Modern_3T_MRI.JPG/440px-Modern_3T_MRI.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Modern_3T_MRI.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The largest single use of liquid helium is to cool the superconducting magnets in modern MRI scanners.</div></div></div>

</p>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:red; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Cryogenics (32%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:green; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Pressurizing and purging (18%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:yellow; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Controlled atmospheres (18%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:fuchsia; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Leak detection (4%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:aqua; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Breathing mixtures (2%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:white; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Other (13%)</div>
</div>
</div></div>
<p>While balloons are perhaps the best known use of helium, they are a minor part of all helium use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stwertka_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-stwertka-63">[63]</a></sup> Helium is used for many purposes that require some of its unique properties, such as its low <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling point</a>, low <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>, low <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">solubility</a>, high <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Chemically_inert" title="Chemically inert">inertness</a>. Of the 2014 world helium total production of about 32 million kg (180 million standard cubic meters) helium per year, the largest use (about 32% of the total in 2014) is in cryogenic applications, most of which involves cooling the superconducting magnets in medical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI" title="MRI">MRI</a> scanners and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NMR" title="NMR">NMR</a> spectrometers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Other major uses were pressurizing and purging systems, welding, maintenance of controlled atmospheres, and leak detection. Other uses by category were relatively minor fractions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs-helium_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-helium-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controlled_atmospheres">Controlled atmospheres</span></h3>
<p>Helium is used as a protective gas in growing <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a> crystals, in <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a> production, and in <a href="/wiki/Gas_chromatography" title="Gas chromatography">gas chromatography</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL.gov_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-LANL.gov-83">[83]</a></sup> because it is inert. Because of its inertness, <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">thermally and calorically perfect</a> nature, high <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a>, and high value of the <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity_ratio" title="Heat capacity ratio">heat capacity ratio</a>, it is also useful in <a href="/wiki/Supersonic_wind_tunnel" title="Supersonic wind tunnel">supersonic wind tunnels</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Impulse_facility" title="Impulse facility">impulse facilities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gas_tungsten_arc_welding">Gas tungsten arc welding</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gas_tungsten_arc_welding" title="Gas tungsten arc welding">Gas tungsten arc welding</a></div>
<p>Helium is used as a <a href="/wiki/Shielding_gas" title="Shielding gas">shielding gas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arc_welding" title="Arc welding">arc welding</a> processes on materials that at welding temperatures are contaminated and weakened by air or nitrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-18">[18]</a></sup> A number of inert shielding gases are used in gas tungsten arc welding, but helium is used instead of cheaper <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> especially for welding materials that have higher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_conductivity" title="Heat conductivity">heat conductivity</a>, like <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_uses">Minor uses</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_leak_detection">Industrial leak detection</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ac-system_2.jpg"><img alt="Photo of a large, metal-framed device (about 3×1×1.5 m) standing in a room." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="939" data-file-width="1563" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ac-system_2.jpg/220px-Ac-system_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ac-system_2.jpg/330px-Ac-system_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ac-system_2.jpg/440px-Ac-system_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ac-system_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A dual chamber helium leak detection machine</div></div></div>
<p>One industrial application for helium is <a href="/wiki/Leak_detection" title="Leak detection">leak detection</a>. Because helium <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffuses</a> through solids three times faster than air, it is used as a tracer gas to detect <a href="/wiki/Leak" title="Leak">leaks</a> in high-vacuum equipment (such as cryogenic tanks) and high-pressure containers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nostrand_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-nostrand-140">[140]</a></sup> The tested object is placed in a chamber, which is then evacuated and filled with helium. The helium that escapes through the leaks is detected by a sensitive device (<a href="/wiki/Helium_mass_spectrometer" title="Helium mass spectrometer">helium mass spectrometer</a>), even at the leak rates as small as 10<sup>−9</sup> mbar·L/s (10<sup>−10</sup> Pa·m<sup>3</sup>/s). The measurement procedure is normally automatic and is called helium integral test. A simpler procedure is to fill the tested object with helium and to manually search for leaks with a hand-held device.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>Helium leaks through cracks should not be confused with gas permeation through a bulk material. While helium has documented permeation constants (thus a calculable permeation rate) through glasses, ceramics, and synthetic materials, inert gases such as helium will not permeate most bulk metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight">Flight</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goodyear-blimp.jpg"><img alt="The Good Year Blimp" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="182" data-file-width="458" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Goodyear-blimp.jpg/220px-Goodyear-blimp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Goodyear-blimp.jpg/330px-Goodyear-blimp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Goodyear-blimp.jpg/440px-Goodyear-blimp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goodyear-blimp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Because of its low density and incombustibility, helium is the gas of choice to fill airships such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goodyear_blimp" title="Goodyear blimp">Goodyear blimp</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Because it is <a href="/wiki/Lighter_than_air" title="Lighter than air">lighter than air</a>, <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">airships</a> and balloons are inflated with helium for <a href="/wiki/Lifting_gas" title="Lifting gas">lift</a>. While hydrogen gas is more buoyant, and escapes permeating through a membrane at a lower rate, helium has the advantage of being non-flammable, and indeed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire-retardant" title="Fire-retardant">fire-retardant</a>. Another minor use is in <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocketry</a>, where helium is used as an <a href="/wiki/Ullage" title="Ullage">ullage</a> medium to displace fuel and oxidizers in storage tanks and to condense hydrogen and oxygen to make <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_fuel" title="Rocket fuel">rocket fuel</a>. It is also used to purge fuel and oxidizer from ground support equipment prior to launch and to pre-cool liquid hydrogen in <a href="/wiki/Space_vehicle" title="Space vehicle">space vehicles</a>. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> rocket used in the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a> needed about 370,000 m<sup>3</sup> (13 million cubic feet) of helium to launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL.gov_83-3"><a href="#cite_note-LANL.gov-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_commercial_and_recreational_uses">Minor commercial and recreational uses</span></h4>
<p>Helium as a breathing gas has no <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_narcosis" title="Nitrogen narcosis">narcotic properties</a>, so helium mixtures such as <a href="/wiki/Trimix_(breathing_gas)" title="Trimix (breathing gas)">trimix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heliox" title="Heliox">heliox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trimix_(breathing_gas)#Heliair" title="Trimix (breathing gas)">heliair</a> are used for <a href="/wiki/Deep_diving" title="Deep diving">deep diving</a> to reduce the effects of narcosis, which worsen with increasing depth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thomas_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-thomas-144">[144]</a></sup> As pressure increases with depth, the density of the breathing gas also increases, and the low molecular weight of helium is found to considerably reduce the effort of breathing by lowering the density of the mixture. This reduces the <a href="/wiki/Reynolds_number" title="Reynolds number">Reynolds number</a> of flow, leading to a reduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turbulent_flow" title="Turbulent flow">turbulent flow</a> and an increase in <a href="/wiki/Laminar_flow" title="Laminar flow">laminar flow</a>, which requires less work of breathing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BOCheox21_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-BOCheox21-146">[146]</a></sup> At depths below 150 metres (490 ft) divers breathing helium–oxygen mixtures begin to experience tremors and a decrease in psychomotor function, symptoms of <a href="/wiki/High-pressure_nervous_syndrome" title="High-pressure nervous syndrome">high-pressure nervous syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HungerBennett_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-HungerBennett-147">[147]</a></sup> This effect may be countered to some extent by adding an amount of narcotic gas such as hydrogen or nitrogen to a helium–oxygen mixture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Helium%E2%80%93neon_laser" title="Helium–neon laser">Helium–neon lasers</a>, a type of low-powered gas laser producing a red beam, had various practical applications which included <a href="/wiki/Barcode_reader" title="Barcode reader">barcode readers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laser_pointer" title="Laser pointer">laser pointers</a>, before they were almost universally replaced by cheaper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diode_laser" title="Diode laser">diode lasers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_18-8"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>For its inertness and high <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>, neutron transparency, and because it does not form radioactive isotopes under reactor conditions, helium is used as a heat-transfer medium in some gas-cooled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactors" title="Nuclear reactors">nuclear reactors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nostrand_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-nostrand-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>Helium, mixed with a heavier gas such as xenon, is useful for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermoacoustic_refrigeration" title="Thermoacoustic refrigeration">thermoacoustic refrigeration</a> due to the resulting high <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity_ratio" title="Heat capacity ratio">heat capacity ratio</a> and low <a href="/wiki/Prandtl_number" title="Prandtl number">Prandtl number</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The inertness of helium has environmental advantages over conventional refrigeration systems which contribute to ozone depletion or global warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>Helium is also used in some <a href="/wiki/Hard_disk_drive" title="Hard disk drive">hard disk drives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_uses">Scientific uses</span></h4>
<p>The use of helium reduces the distorting effects of temperature variations in the space between <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">lenses</a> in some <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescopes</a>, due to its extremely low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Index_of_refraction" title="Index of refraction">index of refraction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-21"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup> This method is especially used in solar telescopes where a vacuum tight telescope tube would be too heavy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Helium is a commonly used carrier gas for <a href="/wiki/Gas_chromatography" title="Gas chromatography">gas chromatography</a>.
</p><p>The age of rocks and minerals that contain <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> can be estimated by measuring the level of helium with a process known as <a href="/wiki/Helium_dating" title="Helium dating">helium dating</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_18-9"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc_20-22"><a href="#cite_note-enc-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Helium at low temperatures is used in <a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">cryogenics</a>, and in certain cryogenics applications. As examples of applications, liquid helium is used to cool certain metals to the extremely low temperatures required for <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconductivity</a>, such as in <a href="/wiki/Superconducting_magnet" title="Superconducting magnet">superconducting magnets</a> for <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Magnetic resonance imaging">magnetic resonance imaging</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">Large Hadron Collider</a> at <a href="/wiki/CERN" title="CERN">CERN</a> uses 96 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_ton" title="Metric ton">metric tons</a> of liquid helium to maintain the temperature at 1.9 kelvin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CERN-LHC_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-CERN-LHC-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Inhalation_and_safety">Inhalation and safety</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span></h3>
<p>Neutral helium at standard conditions is non-toxic, plays no biological role and is found in trace amounts in human blood.
</p>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Helium_article_read_with_helium.ogg" title="File:Helium article read with helium.ogg">Effect of helium on a human voice</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="18.370589569161" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Helium_article_read_with_helium.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px"><source data-bandwidth="140280" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/9c/Helium_article_read_with_helium.ogg/Helium_article_read_with_helium.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/><source data-bandwidth="249985" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (250 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9c/Helium_article_read_with_helium.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">The effect of helium on a human voice</div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr/><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a> in helium is nearly three times the speed of sound in air. Because the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_frequency" title="Fundamental frequency">fundamental frequency</a> of a gas-filled cavity is proportional to the speed of sound in the gas, when helium is inhaled there is a corresponding increase in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resonant_frequency" title="Resonant frequency">resonant frequencies</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Vocal_tract" title="Vocal tract">vocal tract</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_18-10"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_frequency" title="Fundamental frequency">fundamental frequency</a> (sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(music)" title="Pitch (music)">pitch</a>) does not change, since this is produced by direct vibration of the vocal folds, which is unchanged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> However, the higher resonant frequencies cause a change in <a href="/wiki/Timbre" title="Timbre">timbre</a>, resulting in a reedy, duck-like vocal quality. The opposite effect, lowering resonant frequencies, can be obtained by inhaling a dense gas such as <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_hexafluoride" title="Sulfur hexafluoride">sulfur hexafluoride</a> or <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hazards">Hazards</span></h3>
<p>Inhaling helium can be dangerous if done to excess, since helium is a simple <a href="/wiki/Asphyxiant_gas" title="Asphyxiant gas">asphyxiant</a> and so displaces oxygen needed for normal respiration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbb_18-11"><a href="#cite_note-nbb-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grass_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grass-157">[157]</a></sup> Fatalities have been recorded, including a youth who suffocated in Vancouver in 2003 and two adults who suffocated in South Florida in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sptimes.com_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-sptimes.com-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbc.ca_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-cbc.ca-159">[159]</a></sup> In 1998, an Australian girl (her age is not known) from Victoria fell unconscious and temporarily <a href="/wiki/Cyanosis" title="Cyanosis">turned blue</a> after inhaling the entire contents of a party balloon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-balloonartists.com.au_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-balloonartists.com.au-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lousballoons.com.au_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-lousballoons.com.au-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bouncetime.co.uk_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-bouncetime.co.uk-162">[162]</a></sup>
Inhaling helium directly from pressurized cylinders or even balloon filling valves is extremely dangerous, as high flow rate and pressure can result in <a href="/wiki/Barotrauma" title="Barotrauma">barotrauma</a>, fatally rupturing lung tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grass_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grass-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slate_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-slate-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>Death caused by helium is rare. The first media-recorded case was that of a 15-year-old girl from Texas who died in 1998 from helium inhalation at a friend's party; the exact type of helium death is unidentified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-balloonartists.com.au_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-balloonartists.com.au-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lousballoons.com.au_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-lousballoons.com.au-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bouncetime.co.uk_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-bouncetime.co.uk-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States only two fatalities were reported between 2000 and 2004, including a man who died in North Carolina of barotrauma in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sptimes.com_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-sptimes.com-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slate_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-slate-163">[163]</a></sup> A youth asphyxiated in Vancouver during 2003, and a 27-year-old man in Australia had an embolism after breathing from a cylinder in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sptimes.com_158-2"><a href="#cite_note-sptimes.com-158">[158]</a></sup> Since then two adults asphyxiated in South Florida in 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sptimes.com_158-3"><a href="#cite_note-sptimes.com-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbc.ca_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-cbc.ca-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> and there were cases in 2009 and 2010, one a Californian youth who was found with a bag over his head, attached to a helium tank,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> and another teenager in Northern Ireland died of asphyxiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Eagle_Point,_Oregon" title="Eagle Point, Oregon">Eagle Point, Oregon</a> a teenage girl died in 2012 from barotrauma at a party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffingtonpost.com_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-huffingtonpost.com-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-today.com_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-today.com-169">[169]</a></sup> A girl from Michigan died from hypoxia later in the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>On February 4, 2015 it was revealed that during the recording of their main TV show on January 28, a 12-year-old member (name withheld) of Japanese all-girl singing group <a href="/wiki/3B_Junior" title="3B Junior">3B Junior</a> suffered from <a href="/wiki/Air_embolism" title="Air embolism">air embolism</a>, losing consciousness and falling in a <a href="/wiki/Coma" title="Coma">coma</a> as a result of air bubbles blocking the flow of blood to the brain, after inhaling huge quantities of helium as part of a game. The incident was not made public until a week later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> The staff of <a href="/wiki/TV_Asahi" title="TV Asahi">TV Asahi</a> held an emergency press conference to communicate that the member had been taken to the hospital and is showing signs of rehabilitation such as moving eyes and limbs, but her consciousness has not been sufficiently recovered as of yet. Police have launched an investigation due to a neglect of safety measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>On July 13, 2017 <a href="/wiki/CBS_News" title="CBS News">CBS News</a> reported that a political operative who reportedly attempted to recover e-mails missing from the Clinton server, <a href="/wiki/Peter_W._Smith" title="Peter W. Smith">Peter W. Smith</a>, "apparently" committed suicide in May at a hotel room in Rochester, Minnesota and that his death was recorded as "asphyxiation due to displacement of oxygen in confined space with helium".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> More details followed in the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">Chicago Tribune</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>The safety issues for cryogenic helium are similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">liquid nitrogen</a>; its extremely low temperatures can result in <a href="/wiki/Frostbite" title="Frostbite">cold burns</a>, and the liquid-to-gas expansion ratio can cause explosions if no pressure-relief devices are installed. Containers of helium gas at 5 to 10 K should be handled as if they contain liquid helium due to the rapid and significant <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a> that occurs when helium gas at less than 10 K is warmed to <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL.gov_83-4"><a href="#cite_note-LANL.gov-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>At high pressures (more than about 20 atm or two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MPa" title="MPa">MPa</a>), a mixture of helium and oxygen (<a href="/wiki/Heliox" title="Heliox">heliox</a>) can lead to <a href="/wiki/High-pressure_nervous_syndrome" title="High-pressure nervous syndrome">high-pressure nervous syndrome</a>, a sort of reverse-anesthetic effect; adding a small amount of nitrogen to the mixture can alleviate the problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HungerBennett_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-HungerBennett-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www-d0.fnal.gov/hardware/cal/lvps_info/engineering/elementmagn.pdf" rel="nofollow">Magnetic susceptibility of the elements and inorganic compounds</a>, in Handbook of Chemistry and Physics 81st edition, CRC press.</span>

<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Weast, Robert (1984). <i>CRC, Handbook of Chemistry and Physics</i>. Boca Raton, Florida: Chemical Rubber Company Publishing. pp. E110. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-8493-0464-4" title="Special:BookSources/0-8493-0464-4">0-8493-0464-4</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=CRC%2C+Handbook+of+Chemistry+and+Physics&amp;rft.place=Boca+Raton%2C+Florida&amp;rft.pages=E110&amp;rft.pub=Chemical+Rubber+Company+Publishing&amp;rft.date=1984&amp;rft.isbn=0-8493-0464-4&amp;rft.aulast=Weast&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHelium"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Rayet, G. (1868) <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180817142616/https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=umn.31951d000083928;view=1up;seq=763" rel="nofollow">"Analyse spectral des protubérances observées, pendant l'éclipse totale de Soleil visible le 18 août 1868, à la presqu'île de Malacca"</a> (Spectral analysis of the protuberances observed during the total solar eclipse, seen on 18 August 1868, from the Malacca peninsula), <i>Comptes rendus</i> ... , <b>67</b> :  757–759.  From p. 758: <i>" … je vis immédiatement une série de neuf lignes brillantes qui … me semblent devoir être assimilées aux lignes principales du spectre solaire, B, D, E, b, une ligne inconnue, F, et deux lignes du groupe G."</i>  ( … I saw immediately a series of nine bright lines that … seemed to me should be classed as the principal lines of the solar spectrum, B, D, E, b, an unknown line, F, and two lines of the group G.)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Captain C. T. Haig (1868) <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=glFJAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA74#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">"Account of spectroscopic observations of the eclipse of the sun, August 18th, 1868,"</a> <i>Proceedings of the Royal Society of London</i>, <b>17</b> :  74–80.  From p. 74:  "I may state at once that I observed the spectra of two red flames close to each other, and in their spectra two broad bright bands quite sharply defined, one rose-madder and the other light golden."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Pogson filed his observations of the 1868 eclipse with the local Indian government,
 but his report wasn't published.  (Biman B. Nath, <i>The Story of Helium and the Birth of Astrophysics</i> (New York, New York:  Springer, 2013), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Zko_Na5IQL8C&amp;pg=PA8#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">p. 8.</a>) Nevertheless, Lockyer quoted from his report.  <a class="external text" href="https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=mdp.39015038750884;view=1up;seq=360;size=150" rel="nofollow">From p. 320</a> of Lockyer, J. Norman (1896) "The story of helium.  Prologue," <i>Nature</i>, <b>53</b> :  319–322 :  "Pogson, in referring to the eclipse of 1868, said that the yellow line was "at D, or near D." "</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lieutenant John Herschel (1868) <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=glFJAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA104#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">"Account of the solar eclipse of 1868, as seen at Jamkandi in the Bombay Presidency,"</a> <i>Proceedings of the Royal Society of London</i>, <b>17</b> :  104–120.  From p. 113:  As the moment of the total solar eclipse approached, " … I recorded an increasing brilliancy in the spectrum in the neighborhood of D, so great in fact as to prevent any measurement of that line till an opportune cloud moderated the light.  I am not prepared to offer any explanation of this."  From p. 117:  "I also consider that there can be no question that the ORANGE LINE was identical with D, so far as the capacity of the instrument to establish any such identity is concerned."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In his initial report to the French Academy of Sciences about the 1868 eclipse, Janssen made no mention of a yellow line in the solar spectrum.  See:
<ul><li>Janssen (1868) <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=hpZDAQAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA838#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">"Indication de quelques-uns des résultats obtenus à Cocanada, pendant l'éclipse du mois d'août dernier, et à la suite de cette éclipse"</a> (Information on some of the results obtained at Cocanada, during the eclipse of the month of last August, and following that eclipse), <i>Comptes rendus</i> … , <b>67</b> :  838–839.</li>
<li>Wheeler M. Sears, <i>Helium:  The Disappearing Element</i> (Heidelberg, Germany:  Springer, 2015), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=5SvABgAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA44#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">p. 44.</a></li>
<li>Françoise Launay with Storm Dunlop, trans., <i>The Astronomer Jules Janssen: A Globetrotter of Celestial Physics</i> (Heidelberg, Germany:  Springer, 2012), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=3YaAup49nqoC&amp;pg=PA45#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">p. 45.</a></li></ul>
However, subsequently, in an unpublished letter of 19 December 1868 to Charles Sainte-Claire Deville, Janssen asked Deville to inform the French Academy of Sciences that :  "Several observers have claimed the bright D line as forming part of the spectrum of the prominences on 18 August.  The bright yellow line did indeed lie very close to D, but the light was more refrangible [i.e., of shorter wavelength] than those of the D lines.  My subsequent studies of the Sun have shown the accuracy of what I state here."  (See:  (Launay, 2012), p. 45.)</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Thomson, William (August 3, 1871). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=IogCAAAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA268" rel="nofollow">"Inaugural Address of Sir William Thomson"</a>. <i>Nature</i>. <b>4</b> (92): 261–278 [268]. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1871Natur...4..261." rel="nofollow">1871Natur...4..261.</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1038%2F004261a0" rel="nofollow">10.1038/004261a0</a>. <a href="/wiki/PubMed_Central" title="PubMed Central">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC2070380" rel="nofollow">2070380</a></span>. <q>Frankland and Lockyer find the yellow prominences to give a very decided bright line not far from D, but hitherto not identified with any terrestrial flame. It seems to indicate a new substance, which they propose to call Helium</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Nature&amp;rft.atitle=Inaugural+Address+of+Sir+William+Thomson&amp;rft.volume=4&amp;rft.issue=92&amp;rft.pages=261-278+268&amp;rft.date=1871-08-03&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC2070380&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1038%2F004261a0&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1871Natur...4..261.&amp;rft.aulast=Thomson&amp;rft.aufirst=William&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DIogCAAAAIAAJ%26pg%3DPA268&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHelium"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-Palmieri_1881-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Palmieri_1881_23-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Palmieri, Luigi (1881). <a class="external text" href="https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=hvd.hnl7mr;view=1up;seq=251" rel="nofollow">"La riga dell'Helium apparsa in una recente sublimazione vesuviana"</a> [The line of helium appeared in a recently sublimated material [from Mt.] Vesuvius.]. <i>Rendiconto Dell'Accademia Delle Scienze Fisiche e Matematiche (Naples, Italy)</i>. <b>20</b>: 223<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">1 May</span> 2017</span>. <q><i>Raccolsi alcun tempo fa una sostanza amorfa di consistenza butirracea e di colore giallo sbiadato sublimata sull'orlo di una fumarola prossima alla bocca di eruzione. Saggiata questa sublimazione allo spettroscopio, ho ravvisato le righe del sodio e del potassio ed una lineare ben distinta che corrisponde esattamente alla D<sub>3</sub> che è quella dell'Helium. Do per ora il semplice annunzio del fatto, proponendomi di ritornare sopra questo argomento, dopo di aver sottoposta la sublimazione ad una analisi chimica.</i> (I collected some time ago an amorphous substance having a buttery consistency and a faded yellow color which had sublimated on the rim of a fumarole near the mouth of the eruption. Having analyzed this sublimated substance with a spectroscope, I recognized the lines of sodium and potassium and a very distinct linear line which corresponds exactly to D<sub>3</sub>, which is that of helium. For the present, I'm making a mere announcement of the fact, proposing to return to this subject after having subjected the sublimate to a chemical analysis.)</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rendiconto+Dell%27Accademia+Delle+Scienze+Fisiche+e+Matematiche+%28Naples%2C+Italy%29&amp;rft.atitle=La+riga+dell%27Helium+apparsa+in+una+recente+sublimazione+vesuviana&amp;rft.volume=20&amp;rft.pages=223&amp;rft.date=1881&amp;rft.aulast=Palmieri&amp;rft.aufirst=Luigi&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbabel.hathitrust.org%2Fcgi%2Fpt%3Fid%3Dhvd.hnl7mr%3Bview%3D1up%3Bseq%3D251&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHelium"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
